Description of Fisheries Resources Values and a Description of the Development proposal (Provide as a minimum: Species present, type of fish habitat present, description of current riparian vegetation condition, connectivity to downstream habitats, nature of development, specific activities proposed, timelines) This report has been revised following review. Supplementary information is provided in “blue” text. The property at 10750 South Watts Road in Ladysmith, BC is legally described as LOT 20, DISTRICT LOT 272, OYSTER DISTRICT, PLAN 8793, EXCEPT PARCEL A (DD 94199N) THEREOF. The property is approximately 2.3 acres (0.93ha) and is zoned I2 (industrial). The parcel is subject to the following Development Permit Area (DPA) as per the Town of Ladysmith’s Official Community Plan (OCP): DPA 5 – Industrial. The subject parcel is accessible from South Watts Road to the west parcel boundary and is aligned with the Trans Canada Highway on the east parcel boundary. Figure 1 is a site location map. Figure 2 is the development plan. The subject property is currently cleared and undeveloped. The parcel was cleared by the previous owner before putting it on the market. The majority of the parcel is vegetated with patchy grass upon uneven, gravelly soil. The development proposed by Green Civilian Industries is a greenhouse complex comprised of six buildings, two smaller utility buildings, a septic field and parking spaces with landscaping along the frontage and south boundary. An area drainage map has been included as Figure 3. The receiving environment for drainage in the area of the property is Stocking Creek. Stocking Creek (Watershed Code 920-318200) supports chum salmon (Oncorhynchus keta), coho salmon (O. kisutch), rainbow trout (O. mykiss) and both anadromous and resident cutthroat trout (O. clarkii). Figure 4 is the SPEA determination drawing. South Watts Road has a shallow roadside ditch on the west side - the ditch is across the paved road from the subject parcel. Most of the ditch is sloped north draining towards Westdown Road. A short section of the ditch opposite the subject parcel drains south due to topography. The ditch crosses Westdown Road through a CSP culvert which is mostly crushed or filled in on the downstream end. A roughly defined flow path continues through a treed area to join an unnamed tributary of Stocking Creek where it crossed the highway. Previous assessments of this tributary identified a vertical barrier to fish passage on the east side of the highway (hanging culvert). Vegetation along the ditch is limited to grass, scotch broom, Himalayan blackberry and common tansy. The ditch has a limited catchment of stormwater due to topography and appears to only flow during heavy rain events. This ditch has no natural headwater; its flow is seasonal, intermittent and low, it has no habitat attributes to support fish or any other aquatic species and no physical access for fish due to downstream barriers. The ditch is separated from the subject parcel by an asphalt road. The 2m SPEA is comprised of a thin strip of weeds and asphalt. A review of the site servicing civil drawings (Figure 5) show that the road fronting the parcel will need to be constructed to an 8m paved surface with 2m wide gravel shoulders and ditches each side. The road is currently 5m to 6m wide with cracking edges. There is no ditch on the east side of South Watts Road and a ditch cannot reasonably be dug along the downhill neighbouring lot frontage – therefore a cross drainage culvert is proposed and improvements to the west side ditch along approximately 5m length to make it deeper and wider are required. Improvements to the road and drainage will not alter the composition of the SPEA which will still be road shoulder Form 1 Page 3 of 12 Page 159 of 367 FORM 1 Riparian Areas Protection Regulation - Qualified Environmental Professional - Assessment Report and asphalt. Most of the stormwater in the site will enter an engineered storm water system with underground detention and surface detention to meet pre-design flows. Overflow will be disbursed over a wide area to the highway ditch. Wastewater design drawings have been included as Figure 6 which proposed a biofiltration system. Irrigation is proposed to include water re-cycling and rainwater use where appropriate. The east side of the parcel slopes down steeply to the Trans Canada Highway ditch that flows south for approximately 300m. The ditch then flows in an unconfined flow path down a 45% vegetated slope for ~ 50m to a small tributary of Stocking Creek where it then joins with the main channel and flows east under the highway. This slope and lack of channel is a barrier to fish passage. The ditch is a manmade, non-fish bearing ditch that was constructed to drain stormwater from the highway. It discharges into Stocking Creek by overland flow, therefore supporting a fish-bearing watercourse. The ditch is considered non-fish bearing because the steep, vegetated slope that the ditch flows down to join the creek lacks a defined channel and has a slope of 45% which creates a barrier to fish passage. There is no fish habitat within the highway ditch and it is typically dry all summer season. The highway ditch has a sparse canopy along the west bank that is dominated by Douglas-fir (Pseudotsuga menziesii), western redcedar (Thuja plicata) and red alder (Alnus rubra) with minor grand fir (Abies grandis). Red alder saplings adjacent to the ditch are regularly mowed. The understory along the ditch is comprised of salmonberry (Rubus spectabilis), Scotch broom (Cytisus scoparius), Pacific dogwood (Cornus nuttallii), indian plum (Oemleria cerasiformis), salal (Gaultheria shallon), Himalayan blackberry (Rubus armeniacus) and willows (Salix spp.). Within the ditch, the vegetation consists of cattail (Typha latifolia), palmate coltsfoot (Petasites palmatus), common rush (Juncus effuses), creeping buttercup (Ranunculus repens), watercress (Nasturtium officinale) and giant horsetail (Equisetum telmatiea). The west bank riparian area is a steep slope to the parcel and is dominated by Himalayan blackberry with some reed canary grass (Phalaris arundinacea), tansy (Tanacetum vulgare), black cottonwood saplings (Populus trichocarpa), ragwort (Jacobaea vulgaris), Scotch broom and giant horsetail. The riparian area on the east bank adjacent to the highway is nonexistent; the bank is lightly vegetated with patchy terrestrial grass, has exposed sandy soils with gravel and much debris/trash and is bound by the concrete meridians. As per the Riparian Area Protection Regulations (RAPR) detailed assessment methodology – the Streamside Protection and Enhancement Area (SPEA) is determined by using the widest Zone of Sensitivity (ZOS). A 2m ZOS for leaf litter, shade and insect drop (large woody debris is not applicable for non-fish bearing ditches) was determined, resulting in a 2m SPEA for the two ditches assessed in this report. No vegetation removal or disturbance is planned within the SPEAs. No encroachment to the SPEA will occur for this project. The highway ditch setback is within the slope from the parcel to the highway. No disturbance is planned beyond the top of slope overlooking the highway. The S. Watts Road SPEA is asphalt. The SPEA is to be considered a no development area with no soil or vegetation disturbance or dumping. Fish and fish habitat within Stocking Creek are not expected to be negatively impacted by this proposal if all measures are followed by the contractor during the development. Construction of this treatment system will require notification to Ministry of Environment following the AEM Code under the Waste Discharge Regulation, and changes may be necessary if so required by a Director. section of biofilter. Drawn by IPR Trax Developments Ltd. 5 May 2020 R0D2 This irrigation leachate sewage system treatment design is intended to provide equalization of peak flows, attenuation of nitrogen and phosphorous in the leachate, together with removal of suspended solids and some organic contaminants. Design followed a performance based design approach as described in the EGBC Professional Practice Guideline, Onsite Sewerage Systems. The design utilized standard approaches to treatment of greenhouse irrigation leachate, wood chip biofilter and PRB design, rationale retained on file. Materials selection was based on the use of local waste or byproduct materials to improve sustainability. The controlling performance objective for the treatment system has been established as achieving removal of nitrogen to meet established water quality standards, with nitrate N from the biofilter (prior to discharge to the chamber discharge system) of median 15 mg/L or less prior to dilution.This conservative target is based on precautionary protection of the highway road ditch and Stocking Creek (receiving environment), and is based on BC Water Quality Guidelines and the EGBC guideline. Preliminary conceptual design was desktop only and has been based on limited information, and is to be refined at detail design. Source control and monitoring will be recommended, with actions to be defined for exceedences. Target system life prior to media replacement in the biofilter and PRB has been balanced to a target of >15 years. The riparian vegetation along the highway ditch is dominated by shrubs and the small stand of trees that exists in the riparian area does not exhibit any signs of danger trees. The riparian area of the S. Watts Rd ditch is comprised of weeds and asphalt. Staff are working on developing a phased approach to reopening the pool, working with Covid protocols and BCRPA guidelines. Softball, Football, Baseball and Soccer groups have worked through these guidelines and have or are currently using Town facilities. The Fitness Centre at the FJCC has reopened by appointment only. The Fall Activity Guide has been released, with classes ranging from art to fitness available for the public to enjoy. So far the classes have been well received, with patrons happy to be back in the facility. The Machine Shop remains closed to tenants and the public with seismic upgrades and foundation work continuing. Staff, in partnership with the Cowichan Trails Society, worked with the Ladysmith youth regarding bike trails located behind Brown Drive Park. Town staff and volunteers rebuilt the entire site, with appropriate signage, to create a safe outdoor space. So far these trails have been well received by bike riders of all ages. The CTSS is offering a trail building workshop for in October which will teach youth how to safely build trails and do maintenance on existing trails. This workshop is already full, with plans to possibly add a second date. Local service clubs are working together, with the Town, to build an accessible walkway around the Forrest Field turf at Lot 108. This involves adding width to the existing walkway as well as an asphalt lane from the main parking lot to the field. Phase 1 of this project, involving the backside and washroom side of the field, is expected to be completed this week. The Ladysmith Golf Club has remained open during Covid and has provided a great opportunity for locals to remain active outside. The Town has worked with the Golf Club to relocate the hole at #5 after receiving concerns from a neighboring home owner. Page 189 of 367 INTRODUCTION/BACKGROUND: The Town’s Procedure Bylaw No. 1666 stipulates that regular Council meetings are held on the first and third Tuesday of each month, unless the meeting falls on a statutory holiday. Committee of the Whole Meetings are held the second Tuesday of every second month. The Community Charter requires local governments to annually notify the public of the schedule of regular Council meetings before December 31st for the coming year. The notice will be published in two consecutive editions of The Chronicle newspaper, and posted on our Town notice board as well as on our website and social media. The notice will also include reference to Committee of the Whole meetings. Meetings may be cancelled or rescheduled throughout the year as required, provided that sufficient notice of the change in schedule is issued. A colour coded calendar is attached for Council's convenience which includes regular Council meeting dates, Committee of the Whole dates, statutory holidays and annual conference dates. When planning the annual Committee of the Whole Meetings, staff found that the September 14 meeting is scheduled the same week that the majority of Council would be attending the UBCM Convention. Staff have therefore moved the meeting to Wednesday, September 28. In 2021, dates of annual conferences are as follows: Association of Vancouver Island & Coastal Communities April 16-18 (Nanaimo) Federation of Canadian Municipalities June 3-6 (Montreal) Union of BC Municipalities September 13-17 (Vancouver) ALTERNATIVES: Council can choose to: 1. Approve the schedule as outlined; or 2. Consider amending the meeting schedule. Council should be aware that changing meetings to a different time or day of the week will require an amendment to the Council Procedure Bylaw, which includes a public notice period before the amendment is adopted. It is therefore recommended that, should Council wish to consider changing the meeting schedule, the proposed schedule be adopted and advertised first, and alternatives be considered in 2021. CS 05/01/2017 That Council direct staff to make a funding application to the Federal Gas 2017Tax Fund Strategic Priorities Fund – Capital Infrastructure Project Stream 140 for the Machine Shop Restoration for up to $2.25 million dollars in grant funding, and support the Town administration with overseeing the management of this grant if successful. CS 02/09/2017 That Council direct staff to proceed with an application for a grant of 2017$100,000 toward the restoration and repairs of the Machine Shop. 029 INTRODUCTION/BACKGROUND: Over the years there have been many discussions regarding the upgrade of the Machine Shop. Numerous grants have been applied for, but the latest $1.7m grant from the Strategic Priorities UBCM program was the only successful one. This grant funding was allocated to seismic upgrades, though additional funds are required to complete the electrical and mechanical systems, and complete the envelope with energy efficient windows, siding and doors. Staff have worked with a grant writer to complete another funding request to the Province for grant monies to complete this last portion of the upgrade, which is necessary to allow users, both new and previous tenants, access to the Machine Shop. Background on work to date: The Town used the $1,752, 553 in grant funding to stabilize the Machine Shop structure to withstand wind and seismic forces and to address ongoing concerns with the roof and structural work to the foundation. In order to complete these upgrades some of the Machine Shop’s historical defining characteristics were removed to complete the necessary structural work. The intent is to add these features in some form at a later date as funding becomes available. Details on the work completed: Page 195 of 367 The project budget was initially developed with no knowledge of the existing foundation conditions of this building. The structural upgrades originally planned anticipated that there was a competent foundation existing. Once the contractor was hired and the construction was to proceed, the structural and geotechnical engineers advanced their explorations, which revealed that the timber piles that supported the building were rotten and the solid bedrock was between 5- and 8-feet down, much further than originally assessed in the 2014 Omicron report. It was determined by the engineer, architect and the contractor that the best way to proceed was to excavate beneath the building, creating an entirely new foundation of reinforced concrete. This was completed in sections along each of three, 200-foot-long supporting walls. This phase utilized a considerable amount of the available funding provided by the UBCM Infrastructure Grant. As work progressed, items identified in the grant were prioritized and planned within the remaining funds. Work completed to date, including demolition, includes improvement in:  structural and geotechnical;  architectural;  mechanical plumbing and sprinklers; and  relocation of electrical service To date there are:  14,150 board feet of Douglas Fir timbers used to secure the structure and support the long span of the roof in the western half of the building;  14,000 lbs of custom steel plates and bracket timber connectors used to secure the existing structure;  682 Cubic meters ready-mix concrete supply for foundations and slab replacement; and,  22,000 kg of reinforcing steel installed for foundations. Necessary items required for occupancy of the Machine Shop: In order to meet current building code compliance and occupancy standards there are outstanding items that will be required to allow previous tenants to reoccupy their spaces within the Machine Shop. This next phase consists of alterations to the existing industrial building that was formerly occupied by a mix of arts, culture and heritage type tenants. Building Envelope In general, the intent of the building envelope is to retain the character-defining elements of the exterior while addressing thermal performance and protection of the overall structure.  Retrofit or replace all windows to improve thermal performance.  Replace metal cladding and flashing on all exterior walls.  Install panels on the north and south walls that replicate the large sliding doors.  Replace all necessary street-side man doors, thereby improving safety, fire egress, security and thermal performance. Structural Page 196 of 367 The existing wall on the west side of the building is without framing or sheeting. Currently the metal cladding is only attached to the large timber framing. To rectify code deficiencies and to improve the thermal efficiency, the wall will require framing, exterior sheeting and vapor barrier before the new cladding can be installed. Framing to define the various occupied space within the building needs to be completed, as does the framing of the main central mechanical room that will house the proposed HVAC system. Electrical The existing electrical distribution exhibits some Canadian Electrical Code deficiencies, including missing disconnecting means and insufficient clearances. Some of the existing mechanical infrastructure is no longer in use and the renovations will result in more of this equipment becoming obsolete. Addressing emergency components of electrical will also allow for adequate fire safety and evacuation plans addressing egress and exits based on tenant layout and future uses.  Removal of obsolete and deficient distribution equipment in the electrical room  Upgrade panel boards and establish a single BC Hydro Meter I in the electrical room  Additional fire and smoke detection at all stairwells and alarms  Installation of emergency exit signs  Installation of emergency lighting  Upgrade the existing to meet the requirements of the revised layout  Installation of house electrical panel and separate meters for tenants Mechanical System The goal of the mechanical system design will be to provide plumbing and mechanical systems which are durable, easy to maintain and provide a high level of energy efficiency, comfort, air quality and operational safety.  Replacement of water distribution piping to meet CSA standards  Replacement of domestic hot water tanks  Addition of new washroom on ground level that will meet accessibility guidelines  Installation of new HVAC system to suit tenant types  Upgrade existing fire suppression sprinklers to meet the needs of the new layout Boardwalk The existing boardwalk was removed during the necessary foundation work on the east side (front) of the building. In order to achieve an accessible building that aligns with the future plans of the Arts and Heritage Hub, a new boardwalk will need to be constructed. Items NOT included at this time but need to be considered for this and future area development Infrastructure Upgrades Page 197 of 367 New services at Oyster Bay Drive to connect to the building and future development of the Arts and Heritage Hub will need to be considered, given that the ultimate use of this building will include businesses with a higher demand on those services. This work will include:  Sewer lift station to serve the Machine Shop and future new construction  Water line (currently being addressed) Remediation of Contaminated Material Clean-up and remediation at the site is required due to the contaminated soil that was removed from the site to allow for the necessary foundational work. This large pile of earthwork soil remains at the site; the Town will need to establish a plan for this material. ALTERNATIVES: Council can choose to: 1) Utilize the Machine Shop space as a new City Hall – this option comes with funding of $475k which represents restricted funds placed into a reserve “solely for the acquisition of land and construction of a new Municipal Office Building” 2) Short-term borrow the funds to complete the described work and rent the space at commercial rates to pay for the debt. 3) Close the building. FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS; Grant funding is necessary to complete this next phase of the project. The Investing in Canada Infrastructure Program – Community, Culture and Recreation program allows for funding of up to 73.33% for this project, leaving the Town to come up with ~27% or $1.132 million. There are funds in the Real Property Reserve that could be used to fund this project, though these monies are committed to fund the Waterfront Area Plan. There are some funds available in General Reserves that would make up the difference, and the annual allocation towards infrastructure can also be used for this purpose. Unfortunately, due to stacking rules, Gas Tax funds cannot be used to fund the Town’s portion. LEGAL IMPLICATIONS; It is noted that the deadline to apply for this grant was October 1 st. A late resolution is permitted by the funder. If Council decides not to apply for this grant, the application will be pulled. CITIZEN/PUBLIC RELATIONS IMPLICATIONS: Bringing the building to completion will provide the opportunity for previous tenants and the public to safely access the building. INTERDEPARTMENTAL INVOLVEMENT/IMPLICATIONS: Much of the work to submit the grant application has been done. Parks, Recreation & Culture will continue to oversee the renovations at the Machine Shop. BTY Group CONTENTS 1.0 Intent 1 2.0 Tenant Program and Layout 3 3.0 Schematic Design 9 4.0 General Building Upgrades 15 5.0 Structural Design Report 19 6.0 Mechanical Design Report 89 7.0 Electrical Design Report 99 8.0 Building Code Report 105 9.0 Building Envelope Report 115 Page 202 of 367 Context Plan Page 203 of 367 1.0 INTENT The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Shop Building, currently referred to as the Machine Shop, forms a strategic piece in the vision for the Ladysmith waterfront, as outlined in the 2017 Waterfront Plan prepared by DIALOG. The building forms the anchor for one of the precincts in the plan defined as the “Arts and Heritage Hub”. The Hub is intended to contain a variety of industrial, arts and community users such that a lively, cultural precinct is created at the front door to the waterfront. The Machine Shop is in need of considerable upgrades to give it a long, purposeful life. At present, the building is largely occupied but does have empty, underutilized and inefficient space that could be repurposed. As stated in the Pre-Design Report prepared by HOTSON Architecture Inc. in April 2018 . . . “The Machine Shop has the potential to become a focus in the community, serving as a destination for both local residents and visitors to Ladysmith”. The intent of this Schematic Design Report is to advance the scope of work required for the implementation of the Machine Shop to the next level of design. Due to funding constraints it will not be possible to undertake all of the suggested changes to the building recommended in this report as a single phase of construction. The top priorities at this time are to stabilize the structure of the building to withstand both wind and seismic forces; to reinforce the roof structure to deal with the longer than permitted spans; and, to upgrade the roof with a plywood diaphragm, insulation and waterproof membrane. View of the front of the Machine Shop Page 204 of 367 1 Existing South-East Facade Existing entry door on South-East Facade Page 205 of 367 2 2.0 TENANT PROGRAM AND LAYOUT The roster of tenants currently occupying the building includes the following organizations and individuals: • Ladysmith Maritime Society • Ladysmith Arts Council • Independent artists: - John Marston, Stz’uminus First Nation artist - Dennis Brown The consultant has continued to meet with representatives of these tenant groups to discuss the needs and interests of users, and to brainstorm future opportunities both within the building and the wider Arts and Heritage Hub. Concerted support was expressed by the tenants for the sharing of spaces such as classrooms and retail space as well as the idea of adding new tenants that would provide a commercial component to the mix. This is an important strategy for two reasons. First, by including some commercial space in the project, people will be more likely to find the site attractive. The first priority in this regard would be the addition of a small food services component as there is nothing on the site to serve tenants or the visiting public. It is also recommended that a use such as a brewery with tasting room, or a full brewpub, be included in the project at the earliest possible time. More space in the building should be made available to small, independent artists. People love watching things being made. Artists on site will create a draw, allowing people to watch them at work, and giving the artists the opportunity to sell their wares on premise. The Town of Ladysmith will, in turn, share in the proceeds from both artists and commercial tenants through rent, or a percentage of gross sales, as well as covering the annual operating costs. There are also four existing buildings north of the Machine Shop that can add to the creation of the Arts and Heritage Hub. In particular, the railway restoration and artifact displays and the boat building and restoration functions currently on site should be developed as key attractions. When funding becomes available, the historic buildings can be renovated for these activities that will be of real interest to the public. And, over time, there is the potential to add other new buildings with related activities to build a strong “sense of place” for the precinct. The following diagrams have been prepared to illustrate the potential reallocation of space in the Machine Shop for the existing tenants. The first two plans show the existing layout of tenants. The three options that follow make different assumptions about the specific fire exiting requirements from the second floor that can affect the tenant type and layout. These options are to be reviewed by Town Council leading to a preferred direction for the tenant allocations. Page 206 of 367 3 EXISTING MAIN FLOOR Unit H Arts Ccl. Unit G Arts Ccl. Unit F J. Tenant Layout 2 best responds to the accommodation of existing tenants while adding some new blood into the mix. Key features of this plan include: 1 The south half of the Backshop area, with its high-volume space, is ideally suited to a brewery manufacturing operation possibly with a tasting room function. This occupancy would be the goal over the longer term. In the interim, John Marston would remain in this space. 2 In the north half of the Backshop the Ladysmith and District Heritage Society would add a component to the building with railway display. Here, the locomotive currently being restored could be shown in an environment that will protect it from deterioration due to weather. Other rail artifacts would also be displayed in the space by the LDHS. 3 At street level, the LMS Marine Heritage Centre, artist studios and Arts Council spaces are retained but a new, shared retail shop is proposed where tenants can market their artistic works. 4 On the second floor the Arts Council remains as the principal tenant but with shared spaces for a classroom, boardroom and LMS offices. This change has the added value of resolving the exiting from the second floor where a single tenant only requires two open stairs leading to ground floor exit doors. One of the deficiencies in the building is the lack of washrooms for both staff and the public. Currently, the Washroom Building north of the Machine Shop contains both male and female facilities. The recommended plan proposes that new shared washrooms be built, centrally located in the Machine Shop, accessible to both staff and the public. This solution will result in the closure of the Washroom Building that can then be converted into another use. Page 212 of 367 9 EXTERIOR DESIGN In general, the design intent is to retain the character-defining elements of the exterior of the Machine Shop. This approach refers to the retention of the scale and design of windows and doors, the use of metal cladding on exterior walls, and the broad roof overhang along the east façade. Over time some of the original elements of the building exterior have been altered, such as the location and size of windows and doors. Also, some of these components are no longer functional. For example, the large swing doors on the Backshop are difficult to open, leak air badly, and are rotting. These need to be replaced with new construction so that they can become a functioning part of the building operation. During the early stage of construction, all windows should be tested for rot to determine whether they can be restored or need to be replaced. If the lifespan of the building is to be another 100 years, then it is likely best practice to replace all doors, windows and exterior cladding over the long term of the project. The following elevation drawings illustrate the recommended upgrades to the exterior facades of the buildings. A schedule of window and door types is included. A code analysis has confirmed that the greatest flexibility in tenant selection and location in the building, both short- and long-term, is to treat the entire space as an “Assembly Occupancy”. Building upgrades can be carried out in packages as funding permits. Some tasks must be completed immediately, to deal with the structural issues outlined above, and to accommodate changes in tenant location; others can wait for available funding. The following items summarize the changes that are recommended to realize the vision for the project. Those items marked with an asterisk (*) are recommended for immediate consideration. Page 224 of 367 0037-078 Page 1 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited PREAMBLE This report is to be considered a concept study of the structural aspects of this building with emphasis on the lateral load resisting systems. The next steps for this structure would include working design covering a detailed structural upgrade of the building. This concept report is developed in concert with proposed architectural plans and layout. There are structural retrofit alternatives, especially toward lateral load (seismic) upgrades and maintaining historical attributes that need to be considered together with comprehensive and more accurate cost estimates. Consideration toward construction phasing and related impact to stakeholder and / or user requirements are important to developing a final design. Structural concept drawings are located in Appendix A. A structural condition report was completed in January 2019 which is found in Appendix B. This report indicates that there are some rotted roof beam areas which affect the basic capacity for snow load resistance. Depending on when the final structural upgrade construction occurs, this structure is not safe to occupy when there is a likelihood of any applied snow load. Included in Appendix C is a structural drawing for temporary shoring. Please consider the following as ball-park figures only and it is highly recommended that a cost consultant verify these figures. With the above in mind, we feel a seismic retrofit of this nature could cost in the realm of $100 per square foot for a 60% of current code upgrade, meaning that dollars in the $1.3 million range could be expected. There are some variables such as deterioration levels that could make a significant impact on costing either plus or minus for this stage of design development. However, perhaps some of the structural costs could be justified in consideration that the roof replacement is needed anyway. Roof replacement alone could cost in the order of $15 per square foot, or in the order of 15% of the retrofit costs. The preliminary analysis was carried out by assuming Seismic Site Classification “C” and other related geotechnical parameters for the Town of Ladysmith. Please see Appendix D WSP Geotechnical Report: Town of Ladysmith, seismic upgrade to 610 Oyster Bay Drive, Geotechnical desktop study with Recommendations at the end of this report. 1. 1.1. BACKGROUND Existing Structural Framing The existing two storey 24,000 ft2 wood frame building is approximately 200 ft. long x 66 ft. wide and for the purposes of this report the front of the building (2 storey side) is the east elevation. This historical building was constructed in 1943 as a Railway Shop building, accessed by rail tracks through the end wall openings, and today is used for a variety of commercial spaces including Museums, Art Galleries and a Carving Shop. The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Structural Engineering Pre-Design Report Gravity & Seismic. Page 225 of 367 0037-078 Page 2 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited The building is divided into a two storey space on the east measuring approximately 25 ft. x 200 ft. and a single storey high volume space on the west measuring approximately 41ft. x 200 ft. Building construction is of wood frame incorporating solid plank decking on the roof and floors. The roof of the high volume space on the west is plank decking spanning to solid heavy timber purlins spanning north/south which are supported by heavy timber beams spanning east / west. The roof of the two storey space on the east is plank decking spanning to heavy timber purlins spanning north / south which are again supported by heavy timber beams spanning east/west. The floor of the two storey space is plank decking supported by timber floor beams spanning east/west. The previous paragraph describes the condition where the primary beams lap over the interior wall. The roof and floor beams are supported by heavy nominal size 12” x 12” timber columns on the east and west elevations and along the interior demising wall between the single and two storey spaces. These columns are founded on concrete spread footings. The bay spacing of heavy timber bay elements is approximately 10 ft. o/c in the north / south direction. Some of the columns on the east side have been repaired in the past due to rot at the base. It is assumed that exterior walls along the east and west and the interior demising wall are infill walls and are non-load bearing. It is also assumed that the building walls are founded on concrete strip footings. The main floor of the west single storey area is concrete slab-on-grade and the main floor of the two storey east section is a combination of concrete slab-on-grade construction and wood floor. It is our understanding that the concrete slab in the east area is a replacement to the original wood floor on timber sleepers as a result of deterioration of the wood floor due to rot. The lateral load resisting system in the north/south direction is a series of timber Vbracing on the east and west exterior walls and the interior demising wall between the single storey and two storey spaces. The lateral load resisting system in the east/west direction is via pairs of 3” x 12” diagonal timber braces aligning with the exterior timber columns on the west elevation and connected at ground level to creosote treated 9” to 10” diameter driven timber piles. These piles are driven into the old railway embankment that supports the railway roadbed. It is noted that we have reviewed the report prepared by Omicron dated April 2, 2014, entitled “610 Oyster Bay Drive Property Condition Assessment” in our assessment of the structure. 2. SITE REVIEWS Our site reviews on April 13, 2018 and September 27, 2018 included visual reviews only of interior and exterior areas that were accessible and there was no testing for timber deterioration in any of the timber elements or concrete testing of column footings or concrete slab-on-grade. The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Structural Engineering Pre-Design Report Gravity & Seismic. Page 226 of 367 0037-078 Page 3 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited The building design, detailing and construction is typical of heavy industrial buildings in the early 1900’s incorporating plank floor and roof sheeting, and heavy timer purlins, beams and columns with timber column corbels supporting beams and heavy timber V-bracing. It is noted that some areas of the original wood floor on the two storey and single storey area have been replaced with concrete slab-on-grade construction and some areas in the two storey sections on the east are showing deterioration and settlement of the wood plank floor which is supported by wood sleepers on dirt. Also there is some sever timber rot in the diagonal braces on the west elevation where they connect to the treated piles. A thorough inspection of the timber elements for deterioration was undertaken in January 2019 which identifies areas in the roof, walls and floor where localized rot has taken place. It is observed that water ingress has been an ongoing problem over the years as evidenced by staining of roof beam, purlins and decking elements. In addition some of the roof beam elements are twisted and all the long span 12” x 18” beams (spanning 41 ft.) are sagging at least 3” to 5” under dead load only. See Appendix B for the Materials Condition Report. 3. 3.1. STRUCTURAL ANALYSIS OF EXISTING STRUCTURE Gravity Loads Upper Interior Floor Purlins (25 Ft. Span) The upper floor of the two storey area is constructed of nominal 4 x 12 @ 24” o/c purlins (or floor beams) assumed to be equivalent to Douglas Fir #1 (D-Fir#1) grade. A design check on these beams indicate that they would be sufficient to support an office type live load of 2.4 kPa (50 PSF) but would be 100% overstressed for a floor with a live load of 4.8kPa (100 PSF) which would be required for Assembly occupancy use in accordance with the BC Building Code. However, deflection of this floor under a live load of 50 PSF does exceed the recommendations for directly applied gypsum board ceilings. 3.2. Roof Beams Supporting Gravity Loads (41 ft. span) The long span roof beams are nominal 12” x 18” and are assumed to be equivalent to Douglas Fir #1 for analysis purposes. However, these beams are water stained and twisted and so structural properties may not necessarily be equivalent to D-Fir#1. Analysis indicates that the west side long span roof beams do not have adequate strength to support current Code requirements for snow loads. This is also evidenced by noticeable sagging of the beams under dead load only. For this reason, these beams must be shored prior to anticipated snow load. Where these beams are used as seismic drag struts, new brackets will be required at the interior wall due to the lap configuration between the east and west spans. See Appendix A. The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Structural Engineering Pre-Design Report Gravity & Seismic. Page 227 of 367 0037-078 Page 4 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited 3.3. Columns (24 ft. high) 3.3.1. West Wall Columns: West wall columns are nominal 12” x 12” and equivalent to D-Fir#1 grade. The weaknesses identified in the preliminary analysis are two-fold; 1. The connections to the roof beams are likely not adequate for wind or seismic forces. 2. The braces on the west side are connected at approximately 2/3 height and these braces may impart substantial forces into these columns from seismic ground motions. This is a concern due to the connection of the brace to piles located on a steep embankment (see recommendations below). 3.3.2. Centre Wall and East Wall Columns: As for the west walls, these nominal 12” x 12” columns are assumed to be equivalent to Douglas Fir #1 grade. They are knee braced to the underside of the roof beams above for lateral support in the long direction of the building. This bracing system will not be adequate for Code level ground motions. The center and east columns are supported partially against buckling by the second floor beams so the gravity loads can likely be adequately resisted with some upgrading once the connections are verified. 3.4. Lateral Load Resisting Systems 3.4.1. East-West (short direction): As noted previously, the lateral load resisting system consists of external braces from the west columns, to piles located in the adjacent railway embankment. This system is not adequate for current Code level seismic forces. Also these braces, as previously noted, may impart substantial forces into the columns. Essentially, the lateral load system is inadequate and does not meet seismic requirements. Analysis indicates that a new lateral load resisting system is required that is designed in concert with future development plans (see recommendations below). 3.4.2. North-South (long direction): The interior center demising wall between the single storey and two storey in the north/south direction is currently by heavy timber V-bracing (knee bracing). There are essentially 3 lines of knee bracing at each of the column lines in the long direction at the underside roof level. A preliminary analysis indicates that these braces will need to be upgraded or replaced with either steel cross bracing or shear walls. A visual review of the knee bracing on the west side indicates that connections are not adequate and seismic drift will likely be excessive. Again, these braces will need to be upgraded or replaced with steel cross bracing (see recommendations). The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Structural Engineering Pre-Design Report Gravity & Seismic. Page 228 of 367 0037-078 Page 5 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited 3.4.3. Diaphragms The roofing was identified in the Omicron Report as “critical” so roofing and diaphragm upgrades are required. The work is also required for seismic and lateral load resistance upgrading. The second floor diaphragm will likely need to be upgraded as described in Recommendations. These upgrades are required because plank decking in itself does not provide adequate diaphragms for lateral loads to be transferred to brace bay or shear wall elements. 4. STRUCTURAL UPGRADING (CHANGE OF USE) Most Municipalities require structural upgrading on existing buildings when there is a “change of use and occupancy” as defined in the Code. If the high volume single storey space changes from F-2 Occupancy (Industrial) to A-2 Occupancy (Assembly) then the building would need to be upgraded seismically to at least 60% of current Code requirements. The second floor is currently rated for 50 PSF floor live load but an assembly occupancy will require an upgrade to 100 PSF (see commentaries and recommendations below for further details). 5. 5.1. RECOMMENDATIONS Immediate Shoring of Roof Beams The 12 x 18 nominal roof beams spanning 41 feet do not have adequate capacity to support any snow load. There are essentially two (2) options: 1. Provide shoring as per shoring drawing in Appendix C. 2. Install the final bracing system as shown in Appendix A. In any event, this work must be done before snow is anticipated, probably before November 2019. 5.2. Roof Beams (gravity loads): Beams on the west side (long span) are not adequate for snow loads and are noticeably sagging. The proposed framing system shown in Appendix A will effectively reduce the roof beam span while also providing some improved lateral stability. There are several options for the bracing system. 1. Steel HSS column bracing. 2. Steel wide flange bracing. There are other options such as replacing the beams or adding additional beams and spandrels. However, the bracing recommended above provides a load path to lateral shear wall and bracing elements. See Appendix A. With respect to deflection, old wood long span beams sag due to wood creep over time. The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Structural Engineering Pre-Design Report Gravity & Seismic. Page 229 of 367 0037-078 Page 6 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited 5.3. Second Floor Beams (gravity loads): The Herold Engineering Report of April 23, 2018 identified that the second floor can only support a 50 PSF load but only if the beams are Doug-Fir #1 grade and in good condition. Even with #1 grade confirmed, the deflections are at L/327 for 50 PSF which may be acceptable for office use but not good for an assembly load. The existing system of beams and purlins will require upgrading if it is desired to change the occupancy to support the assembly loads of 100 PSF. This can be done by installing beams in-between the existing. 5.4. Lateral East-West: Remove the exterior braces and construct a new lateral load system. There are several alternatives: 5.4.1. Option 1: Steel Brace Frames. Constructed from each column base to a point along each roof beam at each end. See Appendix F. (Part of the roof beam upgrade, see clause 5.2 above) 5.4.2. Option 2: Concrete, reinforced masonry or perhaps wood shear walls. The choice above between steel braced frames or shear walls may depend on the architectural layout and cost. Foundations for the above will be necessary but geotechnical requirements will need to be considered and may have a significant impact on the design choice. A preliminary review of the Hotson Architectural layout indicates that there are several locations for brace frames or shear walls. However, it would be necessary to line up some of the upper and lower walls. It may impact the layout somewhat but some coordination is warranted. It is likely that a detailed analysis of the exterior end walls for out-of-plane forces such as wind, will result in a requirement for some new columns and wind girts. However, these columns could be incorporated into the requirement for new brace frames or shear walls. New foundations will be required in either brace frame or shear wall locations interior and exterior. A concept for new foundation footings is shown on drawings. See Appendix A. A question of whether there is a requirement to maintain the railway tracks through the end walls will need to be addressed. 5.5. Lateral North-South There are several options for upgrading the north south lateral system. A preliminary review of the knee brace system for the east and center walls indicates that the knee braces could be upgraded, including connections that meet at least 60% of the current Code level forces. However, a detailed analysis is required to verify seismic drift and diaphragm force transfer. The east face has numerous windows, but the owners and/or Architect may accept having some braces appearing through the windows. The second floor level does not have adequate bracing, so added bracing of wood or steel or shear walls of either concrete, masonry or plywood will be required. The Comox Logging and Railway Co. Structural Engineering Pre-Design Report Gravity & Seismic. Page 230 of 367 0037-078 Page 7 of 8 Herold Engineering Limited For the west side, preliminary analysis indicates that alternatives for seismic upgrade are required and will likely include constructing new brace frames or shear walls. 5.6. Lateral Roof Diaphragms: Preliminary analysis indicates that there is need for some substantial upgrading, including replacement of the roof diaphragm and repair and/or upgrading of the roof beams. The Omicron Report lists the roof condition as “critical” so it is recommended to include a complete diaphragm upgrade and connections to new lateral load resisting frames. The roof diaphragm will need to transfer the forces into the brace or shear wall elements so connections are an important consideration. If the existing decking is not deteriorated beyond repair, plywood sheathing could be added on top. EPS profiled insulation and new roofing membranes could then be installed above. It will be necessary to keep the lateral load resisting elements spaced so that connections to bracing or shear walls is made cost effective. The recommended roof diaphragm upgrade is to remove the existing roofing down to the wood deck. At this point, the existing decking is reviewed for rot, nailing and connections. If the deck is found to be structurally adequate then 5/8” plywood is applied directly to the wood deck. Use profiled EPS insulation above the plywood to provide positive drainage effectively mitigating the pooling of water due to the sag of the main roof beams below. The roof diaphragm upgrade will include new edge members, blocking, spandrels and drag struts connected to shear resisting elements for seismic lateral resistance. 5.7. Lateral 2nd Floor Diaphragm The second floor diaphragm will also require upgrading including upgraded connections to shear wall or brace frame elements. It is likely that a second floor upgrade would include either plywood over the existing floor or perhaps steel planbracing either below or within the existing floor framing thickness. 5.8. Building Envelope Investigations The building envelope report is covered in Appendix E. Submitted by: HEROLD ENGINEERING LIMITED Greg Beaveridge, P.Eng., Struct.Eng., M.l.Struct.E. Ventilation .................................................................................................................... 7 Page 294 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Mechanical Schematic Design Report Page 3 of 8 1.0 Purpose of the Report The intent of this report is to present the concept design of the mechanical, plumbing and fire protection systems for the purpose of establishing the basis of mechanical design, budget and potential sustainable measures for the project. The report will provide information on proposed mechanical systems which can be reviewed by the client and the design team and assist a cost consultant in confirming the mechanical system budget for the project. 2.0 Design Philosophy The goal of the mechanical system design will be to provide plumbing and mechanical systems which are durable, easy to maintain and provide a high level of energy efficiency, comfort, air quality and operational safety. 3.0 Building Description The Ladysmith Machine Shop project is a proposed renovation of a 19,000 ft2 multi use Heritage building, which will include a Brewery including a commercial kitchen, Carving Shop, LMS Harbour Heritage Centre and office space, several Studio spaces, washroom and amenity spaces on the lower floor. The second floor will include an Arts Council space and a central mechanical room. The existing building is slab-on-grade with exposed wood-frame construction. 4.0 Design Criteria All systems will be designed in accordance with applicable codes, local city bylaws and industry best practices. CODES/Standards: o o o o o o o o o o o o 5.0 2015 National Building Code 2015 National Plumbing Code 2015 National Energy Code for Buildings 2018 British Columbia Building Codes Works Safe BC Standards ASHRAE 90.1-2016 (energy standards) ASHRAE 62.1 (ventilation/air quality standards) NFPA 13-2013 Sprinkler System Standards Local Building Bylaws NFPA 77 Recommended Practice on Static Electricity NFPA 68-2013 Standard on Explosion Protection by Deflagration Venting NFPA 664-2012 Standard for the Prevention of Fires and Explosions in Wood Processing and Woodworking Facilities Plumbing Systems 5.1. Sanitary Drainage: Plumbing and drainage will be provided for all fixtures. Piping will be sloped at a minimum grade of 1%. All drainage piping is expected to be cast-iron piping. Sanitary piping from plumbing fixtures will drain by gravity and connect to a 100mm sanitary service. The location of the site sanitary service connection is expected to be on the north side of the building and will be coordinated with civil plans for connection to the building distribution. Page 295 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Mechanical Schematic Design Report Page 4 of 8 Existing sanitary piping to be abandoned in place where inaccessible, with redundant piping removed where possible. The main level water entry and fire sprinkler entry mechanical room will include a 100mm floor drain required for testing and maintaining the fire suppression system. The second level main central mechanical room will include multiple 75mm emergency floor drains. Commercial kitchen drainage will be run through a below grade grease interceptor prior to connection to the main sanitary system. 5.2. Storm Drainage: Existing storm rainwater leaders from gutters were leaking in a couple of sections during pre-design site review. Maintenance will need to be completed on the gutter system with repair of pipe connections and replacement of piping as required. 5.3. Water Distribution: Domestic water entry with 50mm water service is in the southeast area of the building where the Brewery/Tasting Room is expected to be located. It is expected that the existing 50mm domestic water service pipe can be connected to the new domestic water distribution system inside the building. New distribution of domestic water piping should come from this area. All redundant domestic water piping to be removed. Inside the building, a separate 32mm main domestic water pipe with backflow prevention will be provided to supply the kitchen and brew pub plumbing systems. Location of the backflow prevention device will need to be confirmed for service access. One possible location is the Custodial room on the ground level. Piping for Domestic Hot and Cold water distribution is expected to be Type L copper and for Domestic Hot Water Recirculation Type K copper piping will be provided. Alternatively, combustible Polypropylene or non-combustible Stainless Steel piping may be used. The Kitchen/Brewery and general building separate domestic water cold mains will be distributed from the water entry to all plumbing fixtures with isolation valves provided for each group of plumbing fixtures. A 32mm domestic hot water main will distribute hot water to plumbing fixtures in the Brewery/Kitchen with thermostatic mixing valves for public use fixtures. A hot water recirculation system and pump will also be provided. A 50mm domestic hot water main will distribute hot water to general plumbing fixtures throughout the building with thermostatic mixing valves for public use fixtures. A hot water recirculation system and pump will also be provided. All water distribution piping will be insulated to ASHRAE 90.1-2016 standards. All water distribution piping installed in exposed areas will be provided with canvas or PVC jacketing. Page 296 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Mechanical Schematic Design Report Page 5 of 8 Proposed Plumbing Fixtures All plumbing fixtures will meet appropriate CSA Standards. Fixtures for barrier free use will meet the American Disabilities Act Guidelines and ANSI A117.1 requirements. Refer to architectural plans for number and type of fixtures. Low flow lavatory faucets, kitchen sink faucets, water closets, and showers will be used throughout the building to reduce water and sanitary discharge flow rates. The type and manufacturer of each plumbing fixture will be reviewed carefully with the architect and owner prior to finalizing design. Maximum proposed flow rates for fixtures are as follows; Faucets at 1.9 LPM, Showers at 5.7 LPM, Water closets at 4.2 LPF, Kitchen Sinks at 5.7 LPM 5.4. Fire Protection: The existing fire suppression systems were in fair condition at the time of Rocky Point Engineering’s Pre-Design site review. The sprinkler systems were recently upgraded in 2007 and would only require modification to suite renovated building layout. The main existing fire service line enters the building on the northeast side. It is expected that the sprinkler entry room would remain as is and that the new building layout will accommodate the sprinkler entry location. The existing fire protection system will be modified to suit new tenant spaces in accordance with NFPA-13-2013 “Standard for the Installation of Sprinkler Systems” code regulation. New sprinkler heads are required when the location is modified as reuse of existing heads is not allowed with the latest version of the NFPA-13 code. Fire extinguishers will be provided in cabinets throughout the building as required by NFPA 10 “Standard for Portable Fire Extinguishers”. 5.5. Domestic Hot Water Heating System: A single on-demand natural gas fired high-efficiency tankless water heater will be provided for the general building hot water heating system. A single on-demand natural gas fired high-efficiency tankless water heater with build in storage tank will be provided for the Brewery/Kitchen hot water heating system. 5.6. Natural Gas: This building will have a natural gas service for the high efficiency space heating boilers, high efficiency hot water heaters and gas fired make-up air units. Piping to be schedule 40 black steel. Piping distribution will be at 2psig pressure with a common pressure regulator at each set of appliances. There will be a set of two boilers for space heating, two water heaters serving all building plumbing fixtures and two gas fired make-up air units. Page 297 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Mechanical Schematic Design Report Page 6 of 8 Existing natural gas meters located on the south corner of the building will be replaced with new gas meters. Natural gas meters will need to be relocated to the east corner of the southeast wall of the building. It is expected that separate gas meters will be required for the gas fired make-up air units serving kitchen and Carving/Rail Display and Brewery/Kitchen water heaters. Installation and modification of gas service piping and gas meters will be coordinated with Fortis BC. Seismic gas shutoff valves will also be installed and attached at the point of entry to the building. The seismic gas valves will also be provided with a protective enclosure. 6.0 Heating, Cooling and Ventilation Systems: 6.1. Heating and Cooling Systems A high efficiency Variable Refrigerant Flow (VRF) system will be the main source of heating and cooling for the studio spaces, LMS Harbour Heritage Centre, LMS offices, and Arts Council spaces. A semi custom indoor air handling unit with remote condenser will be the main source of heating and cooling for the Brewery. An indoor gas fired make-up air unit will be the main source of heat for the Carving/Rail Display space. High efficiency gas fired hydronic space heating boilers will provide heating for the Brewery, washroom and amenity spaces and backup heating for studio spaces, LMS spaces and Arts Council spaces. Cooling The Variable Refrigerant Flow system serving studio spaces, LMS spaces and Arts Council spaces will include an 18 ton condensing unit and manufacturer supplied controls. Condensing unit is proposed to be located inside the mechanical room with one wall of the mechanical room acting as a louvre (about 50% free area) to provide required air cooling of the condensing unit. An additional exhaust fan may be required to provide additional airflow around the condensing units. This area of the mechanical room should be walled off from the rest of the mechanical room with service access to provide acoustic and thermal separation. This condensing unit will be complete with a 10 ton and an 8 ton module. Fan coil units will be located in each space serving the ground floor rooms. Fan coil units for the second floor Arts Council space will be located in the mechanical room with supply duct distribution exposed at high level in the Brewery and Carving/Rail Display spaces. Mode selection boxes will be installed to link the refrigerant from the condensing units to the fan coil units. The mode selection boxes allow some zones to be in heating while others are in cooling. This allows for greater efficiency and occupant comfort. Each fan coil unit will have individual zone control and multiple speed settings. A semi custom indoor air handling unit with DX cooling coil will be installed in the mechanical room to provide heating and cooling for the Brewery. A remote 10 ton condensing unit will provide cooling for this system. The condensing unit will be located in the walled off mechanical room space next to the 18 ton condensing unit. Refrigerant piping will connect the condensing unit to the DX coil in the air handling unit. No cooling is expected to be required for the Carving/Rail Display space. Page 298 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Mechanical Schematic Design Report Page 7 of 8 Heating The Variable Refrigerant Flow system described above will also be used for heating the studio spaces, LMS space and Art Council spaces. Two high efficiency low temperature condensing natural gas space heating boilers will be provided to heat amenity spaces and Brewery and to provide backup heating for the studio spaces, LMS spaces and Art Council spaces. The boiler system will include Tekmar controls to integrate the controls of the boilers, pumps and control valves.This system will include a low loss header to provide hydraulic separation, primary and secondary pumps, hydronic distribution piping and control valves. The fan coil units serving the second level Arts Council will be provided with unit mounted heating coils and the Brewery will be heated by a hydronic heating coil in the air handling unit serving this space. It is proposed to provide perimeter hot water convectors to provide heating in the remaining spaces with a forced air heater in the entrance way. Two unit heaters will be located in the Carving/Rail Display space for night setback mode. Hydronic heating water lines will be routed through the building to provide heating through hydronic heating coils in air handling unit and fan coil units in the mechanical room and convectors, force flow heater and unit heaters throughout the building. All hydronic heating water lines will be insulated in compliance with ASHRAE 90.1-2016 requirements. Variable volume pumps will circulate hydronic heating fluid to the building heating systems further increasing building efficiency. 6.2. Ventilation It is recommended to have two energy recovery ventilators (ERVs) to provide outdoor ventilation and exhaust to most spaces. The first ERV will provide outdoor air supply to each of the spaces served by the VRF system (studio units, LMS spaces, Art Council spaces). This unit will also exhaust air from these spaces to maintain air balance. The second ERV will provide outdoor air supply to the Carving/Rail Display make-up air unit and air handling unit serving the brewery. Both the MUA-1 and AHU-1 will be provided with the capability to draw more outdoor air directly from outside as is needed to maintain air balance in the space. The second ERV will provide exhaust from the ground floor washrooms, showers and custodial rooms. Kitchen Ventilation System Commercial kitchen ventilation will require a grease exhaust system including a kitchen hood in compliance with NFPA 96 and a roof mounted up blast exhaust fan. It is expected the exhaust airflow rates will be in the range of 2,400 to 3,000 CFM. A natural gas fired makeup air unit located in the mechanical room will provide tempered makeup air to the kitchen when the kitchen exhaust fan is operational. Page 299 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Mechanical Schematic Design Report Page 8 of 8 Wood Carving Space The wood carving space is expected to require a dust collection system in compliance with Worksafe BC regulations and NFPA 664 (Standard for the Prevention of Fires and Explosions in Woodworking Facilities). A non-recirculating baghouse dust collector with UL listed backflow device and explosion vent panels with flame diversion sized to match the connected woodworking equipment will be provided. The dust collector and ductwork will be electrically bonded as per NFPA 77. General ventilation will be provided by a roof mounted exhaust fan. A natural gas fired make-up air unit located in the mechanical room will provide tempered fresh air when the dust collection system is operation. Principal, Senior Project Manager e. aaron.mullaley@rpeng.ca Page 300 of 367 Page 301 of 367 7.0 ELECTRICAL DESIGN REPORT - RB Engineering Limited Page 302 of 367 99 Ladysmith Machine Shop ELECTRICAL SCHEMATIC DESIGN REPORT Prepared By #4 1850 Northfield Road Nanaimo, BC V9S 3B3 Project No. 18‐2872 March 1, 2019 Page 303 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Electrical Schematic Design Report 1.0 Page 1 of 4 OVERVIEW The project consists of renovations to an existing two‐storey building totalling approximately 19,300 square feet of floor area. The building’s interior partitioning will be renovated to establish small, single‐storey height studio spaces in addition to two approximately 3,500 square foot two‐storey height spaces at the rear of the building. A marina and several small outbuildings are also powered from the main facility’s electrical service. 2.0 POWER SERVICE AND DISTRIBUTION The building’s existing electrical supply consists of a 600 amp, 600 volt three‐phase overhead service fed from a nearby pole mounted transformer bank and terminated in the main electrical room located on the second floor. The marina is fed by a separately metered 300 amp, 600 volt three‐phase sub‐feed from this service. The remainder of the distribution consists of an assortment of infrastructure of varying vintage, including transformers providing both 480 volt and 208 volt power and multiple separately metered sub‐feeds serving the existing tenants. The existing distribution exhibits some Canadian Electrical Code deficiencies including missing disconnecting means and insufficient clearances. Some of this existing infrastructure is likely not in use at this time and the proposed renovations will result in more of this equipment becoming obsolete. As renovations proceed, all existing distribution throughout the facility will be removed and replaced with new as needed to suit the revised interior partitioning and specific requirements of prospective tenants. Proposed revisions to the distribution will include establishing a single meter for all building loads (i.e., everything except the marina sub‐feed) consisting of a 400 amp, 600 volt fused disconnect switch feeding a new 300 kVA transformer with 120/208 volt power on the secondary side (please refer to Appendix A for load calculation details compiling our estimates and assumptions on building power loads). A 1200 amp, 120/208 volt three‐phase distribution board will be provided downstream from which individual services to suit the new tenant spaces will be provided as required. In the future, should additional power be required for either the building or the marina a discrete BC Hydro service for the marina can be explored. Please refer to Appendix B for a sketch illustrating a proposed separate overhead service for the marina (exact configuration to be confirmed pending review and input from BC Hydro). 3.0 TELEPHONE AND COMMUNICATION SYSTEMS The building is currently served by an overhead copper telephone service terminating in a demarcation panel located in an office area adjacent to the electrical room. A new overhead CATV service and an upgrade to a fibre telephone service will be provided and RB ENGINEERING LTD Page 304 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop Electrical Schematic Design Report Page 2 of 4 demarked in the main electrical room. A 2” communication conduit will be extended from the demark to each proposed tenant space to allow for communication services as required. 4.0 LIGHTING All existing interior lighting throughout the building will be removed. New LED lighting will be installed to replace the existing fluorescent fixtures and to suit the revised layouts. Automatic lighting controls consisting of occupancy or motion sensors will be provided as per BC Building Code Part 10 requirements regarding energy efficiency. Common areas lighting upgrades will be completed initially while lighting for rental spaces can be completed by future occupants as part of the tenant improvement process. Existing exterior lighting will be replaced with new LED fixtures when upgrades to the building envelope are undertaken. 5.0 LIFE SAFETY SYSTEMS BC Building Code life safety systems (i.e., fire alarm, emergency lighting and exit signage) will be replaced with new throughout the building to suit the revised layouts as follows: 6.0 ‐ New addressable fire alarm panel c/w initiating devices (pull stations, detectors, etc) and notification appliances (audible horns, strobes, etc) ‐ New LED emergency lighting units c/w batteries as required ‐ New green LED “running man” exit signs SECURITY SYSTEM The existing intrusion system will be upgraded as required to suit the owner’s needs based on the revised layouts. 7.0 SUMMARY In general, the work described in this report can proceed in stages. Removal of obsolete and deficient distribution equipment in the electrical room itself can proceed initially with further demolition occurring in tandem with the remainder of the interior renovations. Establishing a single BC Hydro meter in the electrical room for building loads would also be an early priority, while the installation of new feeders and distribution equipment throughout the building can be staged to match the rate of the renovations as they proceed. Similarly, upgrades to other systems (communication, lighting, life safety and security) can also be completed incrementally to suit the project’s progress.
