We have been asked by Hotson Architecture Inc to prepare the following approach to Building Code compliance for the purpose of demonstrating general compliance with the fire/life safety requirements of the Building Code. This report outlines the approach for proposed renovations and an addition to the existing building at 610 Oyster Bay Drive in Ladysmith, BC at the schematic design stage. This report is based on review of information provided by the client, we have not reviewed the building other than as specifically described in this letter and we have not visited the site. This report addresses fire protection, occupant safety, and access for persons with disabilities; it does not address structural, electrical, mechanical, building envelope, or energy conservation other than as specifically described in this report and relative to Division B, Part 3 of the Building Code. This report should be reviewed in conjunction with drawings included in Appendix A. 2.0 APPLICABLE BUILDING CODE The applicable Building Code is the BC Building Code 2018 and references are to this edition. 3.0 PROJECT DESCRIPTION The project consists of alterations to an existing building currently occupied by a mix of arts type occupancies; galleries, artists’ studios and community offices. The building is two storeys in height, 1360m2 in area, and is sprinklered. The proposed occupancy will remain a mix of community offices and meeting spaces, galleries and artists’ studios and there is a desire to use a portion of the first storey for a restaurant or brew pub occupancy. For clarity we refer to this as the restaurant space. This report describes the Building Code’s approach to its application to existing buildings and provides our interpretation of occupancy classifications. A more detailed report may be necessary at the Building Code stage to create a detailed approach to Building Code compliance regarding a specific approach. 4.0 BUILDING CHARACTERISTICS AND CONSTRUCTION The building’s characteristics, following alterations, will be as follows: Occupancy Building height Building area Construction Sprinklered Construction Group A Division 2 2 storeys 1360m² Combustible Yes 3.2.2.26 Article 3.2.2.26 permits buildings of Group A, Division 2 occupancy two storeys in height and 2400m² in area, sprinklered, combustible construction with ¾h fire resistance ratings at floors. 610 Oyster Bay Drive, Ladysmith, BC March 5, 2019 Page 311 of 367 GHL File 6904.00 Page 1 The operation of the building is unique. The Building Code describes a building or part of a building as a suite and a number of suites may exist in a building as separate entities under the care and control of the lessee with no relationship to each other. The definition of suite in the Building Code describes “a room or series of rooms of complementary use operated under a single tenancy…”. In this case, the building is operated by the Arts Council of Ladysmith and District (ACLD) and provides space for artists in a cooperative arrangement. Although some of the spaces are occupied by individuals, the common facilities are shared, and the building is entirely under the care and control of the ACLD, and operates as a single suite. Some of the spaces in the building could be classified Group D (office or service) and others could be classified Group F, Division 3 (light industrial including carving and brewing of beer) and others Group A, Division 2 (assembly such as gallery, restaurant or pub). Individually most of the spaces that meet the definition of assembly occupancy are small enough that they could be considered Group D. We propose classifying the overall building a single suite of Group A, Division 2 occupancy, and we understand it has historically been classified so, and consider the other uses such as offices, studios and the potential brewing to be subsidiary to the pub or restaurant (assembly) occupancy. The Group A, Division 2 both describes most of the uses in the building and is the most onerous of the potential occupancy classifications. 5.0 APPLICATION OF BUILDING CODE TO EXISTING BUILDINGS There are no specific requirements, under either the Building or Fire Code, to bring existing buildings into compliance with the current Building Code, unless the authority having jurisdiction has determined that there is an inherent threat to occupant safety and has issued an order under the provincial Fire Services Act or changes are proposed that would increase risk. Division A, Sentence 1.1.1.2.(1) requires that “…where a building is altered, renovated, rehabilitated, or repaired, the level of life safety and building performance shall not be decreased below a level that already exists.” In this way, the Building Code makes allowances for existing buildings and enables their useful life to be extended through repairs or renovations. New construction within the existing building and the addition are required to comply with the Building Code and the continued use of existing conditions require a practical approach. Appendix A-1.1.1.1.(1) describes application of the Building Code to existing buildings and states that consideration of the level of safety required, similar to the method for alternative solutions, is necessary. Successful application is a matter of balancing cost with the importance of the requirement to the overall Building Code objectives. The User’s Guides published with the National Building Code are referenced as sources for further information. The User’s Guide commentary on application of Part 3 to existing building states that: “… an appropriate level of performance is considered to be achieved if the level of safety is adequate to ensure the safety of occupants should they be exposed to a hazardous situation. Under these circumstances, some requirements affecting property protection and items that do not directly affect occupants may not meet the standard intended by the current Building Code.” The User’s Guide describes the key elements for consideration as follows:   general day-to-day safety of occupants (design of stairs, guards, handrails etc) knowledge of fire (fire detection and alert) 610 Oyster Bay Drive, Ladysmith, BC March 5, 2019 Page 312 of 367 GHL File 6904.00 Page 2    the ability to move to a safe place (number of exits, travel distance, fire separations) control of fire by building elements (fire separations, closures, firestopping) protection of property (fire compartmentation, sprinklers and spatial separation) Other than protection of property, all of the elements relate to evacuation of the building in an emergency; the focus of the Building Code in existing buildings is occupant safety. 5.1 General Day-to-Day Safety (Stairs, Guards, Handrails) As noted previously, we have not undertaken a site visit and cannot make specific recommendations regarding these items at this stage. We understand the renovations include the reconstruction of the interior stairs serving the second storey and the exterior stair, ramps and elevated boardwalk at the entry. This will require that they meet the current dimensional, guard, handrail, slip resistance and nosing and tactile indicator requirements in Sections 3.4 and 3.8 of the Building Code. 5.2 Knowledge of Fire (Alert) We understand the building is equipped with a fire alarm system. 5.3 Moving to a Safe Place (Egress, Exits) The first storey spaces are served by exterior doors and most are small enough that single egress is permitted. Based on the Group A, Division 2 occupancy a single egress is permitted in a sprinklered space having a maximum area of 200m², travel distance to the egress door of 25m and occupant load of 60 persons. The first storey carving and restaurant space and the second storey Arts Council space are over these limits and require two means of egress. The first storey carving and restaurant spaces are, or will be, served by exterior exits and interior egress doors leading to via a common area to the building’s principal entry. This common area is not a public corridor as defined in the Building Code and travel distance is measured from the furthest point in the carving or restaurant space to the exterior door. All parts of the spaces meet part of the 45m travel distance limit. The second storey is served by two stairs leading to exterior doors at the first storey. These stairs, and the landing spaces at the first storey, are effectively part of the second storey and are required to be separated from the first storey by vertical fire separations having the same ¾h fire resistance rating as the floor. Travel distance is measured from the furthest point in the second storey space, down the stair to the exterior door. All parts of the second storey meet the 45m travel distance limit. 5.4 Control of Fire by Building Elements 5.4.1 Fire Separations The User’s Guide describes control of fire spread by fire separations including closures, firestopping, fire suppression, construction type and control of hazards or combustible contents. In this case, the floor assembly is required to be constructed as a fire separation having a ¾h fire resistance rating. As the building is a single suite, fire separations at rooms are minimal. The second storey is separated from the first storey by the floor and by vertical fire separations at stairs and as described in Sentence 3.2.8.1.(1) at the edge of the second storey floor overlooking the first storey carving and restaurant space. The 610 Oyster Bay Drive, Ladysmith, BC March 5, 2019 Page 313 of 367 GHL File 6904.00 Page 3 following is a general list of fire separations, not all rooms described below necessarily exist in the building. There have been significant changes to the requirements for access in the 2018 edition of the Building Code. The discussion below describes the high points, Section 3.8 should be reviewed for details. There is an option in Sentence 3.8.3.1.(1) to use specific provision in the CSA B651 Standard, Accessible Design for the Built Environment, in lieu of requirements in Section 3.8. In some cases these may be an advantage. Access is required from parking to the principal entry, to individual entries at spaces with exterior access only, and within the building to all spaces accessible to the public and to areas where persons with disabilities could reasonably be expected to work. 6.1 Paths of Travel Exterior walks require a width of 1500mm, a slip resistant surface and level areas at entry doors per Article 3.8.3.3. Walking surfaces are permitted openings up to 13mm with elongated openings perpendicular to the direction of travel, maximum cross slope of 1 in 50 and bevels at changes of level such as at thresholds between 6mm and 13mm high per Article 3.8.3.2. There are additional dimensional and slope requirements for passenger loading zones and curb-cut ramps, if these are provided. Interior paths of travel require a width of 1500mm except this may be reduced to 1100mm between fixtures in retail shops or galleries per Article 3.8.3.2. The requirements for walking surfaces are the same as described above for exterior paths. Ramps require a clear width of 1500mm, a slope of not more than 1 in 12 and level areas at the top and bottom, intermediate landing and changes in direction 1500mm x 1500mm per Article 3.8.3.5. Article 3.8.2.2 describes accessible doors at the building’s principal entry and to individual first storey spaces not accessed from within the building. A power door operator is required at the principal entry door per Sentence 3.8.2.7.(1). 610 Oyster Bay Drive, Ladysmith, BC March 5, 2019 Page 314 of 367 GHL File 6904.00 Page 4 Doors in the interior path of travel are required to be accessible. The first storey carving and restaurant space may be accessed either from the exterior or the interior, it is not necessary to provide access from both. Where doors do not provide access, signage is required to direct users to the accessible door. Doors providing access require a clear width of 850mm (measured from the jamb to the face of door) and clear areas as described in Sentence 3.8.3.6.(11) as follows:   1500mm by the door width plus 600mm at the latching jamb where the door swings toward the user. 1200mm by the door width plus 300mm at the latching jamb where the door swings away from the user. Subsection 3.8.3. includes requirements for height and shape of thresholds, location and operation of door hardware including power door operators, timing of self-closers and separation of doors in series. 6.2 Washrooms Article 3.8.2.8 prescribes at least one universal washroom at the first storey. The other washrooms are within 45m of the universal toilet room and thus, are not required to be accessible. Where showers are provided, one shower in each group of showers is required to be designed to be accessible in accordance with Article 3.8.3.16. The two-story heavy timber frame building is to undergo a series of interior alterations, structural upgrades, fenestration and cladding replacement, and roof renewal. To prepare the report, HEL performed a site review on March 13, 2018. In addition, HEL reviewed the condition assessment report completed by Omicron Architecture Engineering Construction Limited (Omicron) dated April 2, 2014. This brief has been based on the design development drawings prepared by Hotson Architecture Inc. (HA) dated February 11, 2019 and our correspondence with the architect Mr. Hotson. The intent of the design brief is to assist the architect in the design of various building enclosure assemblies with an emphasis on the control of moisture, air, and thermal transfer in addition to precipitation management and to meet the latest edition of British Columbia Building Code 2018 and industry’s best practices to the building envelope. The body of the brief has been divided into three sections. The first section identifies the building’s site condition, the environmental loads, and highlights the building’s existing condition to establish design criteria. The mid-section lists recommended assemblies and special considerations of the integration of functional layers at interfaces of the assemblies. A summary and our closing comments can be found in the last section. 1.0 Site, Climate and Environment The building is located in the waterfront area on a heritage site between the Ladysmith Harbour and the Trans-Canada Highway with six other buildings which are surrounded by scarce vegetation. The east of the building is bordered by a raised footpath which is parallel to an adjacent paved roadway. The north and south of the building has overhead doors providing level access and exits to and from the building. The terrain slopes up steeply along the west side and the Page 319 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop March 20, 2019 Page 2 of 6 4598-003 overgrown vegetation and finished grade appears to be in close proximity to the base of the wall. The climatic data according to appendix C of 2018 BC Building Code is as follows:       Heating degree days below 18 degrees ºC: 3000 Driving rain wind pressure 1/5: 180 Pa Hourly wind pressure 1/10: 310 Pa Hourly wind pressure 1/50: 400 Pa Moisture index: 1.2 Annual total precipitation: 1075mm The location of the building is subject to high precipitation combined with heavy wind events and there is not tall neighboring structures or plantation to provide protection or reduce exposure. The existing building was not built with an established energy code. However, BCBC requires a building that is rehabilitated to comply with the latest energy efficiency standards e.g. ANSI/ASHRAE/IES 90.1(ASHRAE 90.1), National Energy Code for Buildings (NECB) or Energy Step Code. For simplicity, ASHRAE 90.1 2016 as opposed to NECB 2015 or Step Code is adopted as the reference code for energy efficiency. The building is considered to be in ASHRAE climate zone 4 and the proposed occupancy type is non-residential in determining insulation values under the prescriptive path for energy efficiency compliance. The building height is 8.54m and the surrounding is an open terrain in identifying NAFS performance criteria. 2.0 Assemblies 2.1 At-grade assemblies The building’s ground floors are mainly concrete slab-on-grade which border a raised footpath on the east elevation and are at level with accesses and exits on the north and south sides. There is overgrown landscape along the length of the west side where the terrain also slopes steeply up as opposed to good practice of down and away from the building. The bottom termination of the existing cladding is in close proximity with the finished grade which has led to accelerated deterioration of the cladding. The height of the presumed concrete foundation is unknown at this point and should provide sufficient ground clearance to the wood components supported and the new cladding should be kept about 8” away from the finished grade to prevent accelerated deterioration. At the west side, vegetation in close proximity of the building is recommended to be cleared and confirmation of perimeter drainage or installation of new drainage if none exists. A strip of gravel bed can be included around this side to deter plant growth and critters. By ASHRAE, R-15 of rigid insulation e.g. 3” of extruded polystyrene (XPS) insulation should be provided to the perimeter of slab-on-grade floors. The above grade portion of the insulation 3701 Shenton Rd. Nanaimo, BC V9T 2H1 mail@heroldengineering.com (250)751-8558 Page 320 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop March 20, 2019 Page 3 of 6 4598-003 should be faced with concrete to provide protection against UV. The insulation is to extend 24” down or to the top of the footing, whichever is less. In the occasion where wood framing will inevitably be located below the adjacent grade, waterproofing should be provided extending sufficiently up and down. A drainage promotion material e.g. drainage mat is recommended to be placed at the outermost layer of the atgrade assemblies. Through our past experience with remediation completed to the building, the existing wood flooring on the ground floor at the north end of the east wing is showing signs of distress as a result of the deterioration of the substrate which is composed of untreated logs. The floor should be remediated by removing the organic substrate and restoring with materials that are suitable for being in direct contact with ground. 2.2 Wall assemblies The existing walls are composed of infill framing between heavy timber wood posts. The following assembly is recommended for the new walls: Table 1 Wall Assemblies Functional Layers Components Cladding Corrugated metal panels Exterior insulation (optional) Rigid mineral wool Water resistive barrier (also air barrier) Synthetic sheathing membrane Sheathing Plywood Cavity insulation R-20 batt insulation in 2x6 framing Vapour retarder 6-mil polyethylene sheet Wall finish Painted 1/2" gypsum wall board The corrugated metal panels providing the first line of protection against elements are recommended to be 24Ga steel coated with Kynar 500 coating. The profile of the metal panels can have 80% free drainage space and be oriented vertically to qualify for a rain screen assembly. Otherwise install ½” strapping to provide the necessary drainage plane and ventilation space between panels and sheathing membrane. Stainless steel fasteners are recommended to be used for attachment of cladding to maximize the long-term serviceability, considering the proximity of the building to the ocean. In the instance where exterior insulation is used and strapping/furring for rain screen is fastened through the insulation, loads exerted on the cladding should be transferred back to the strapping and in turn the studs by lining up the strapping with the studs. Continuous exterior insulation made of high-density rigid vapour permeable mineral wool is optional and is highly recommended if the construction budget permits. The advantages 3701 Shenton Rd. Nanaimo, BC V9T 2H1 mail@heroldengineering.com (250)751-8558 Page 321 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop March 20, 2019 Page 4 of 6 4598-003 include a dimensionally stable, non-combustible, highly permeable and consistently performing thermal control layer which would help maintain the temperature of the subsequent wood framing and reduce the risk of interstitial condensation. HEL recommends a dual air barrier system for the walls consisting of a primary air barrier system constructed of either vapour permeable self-adhered membrane or taped and sealed synthetic sheathing paper on the exterior and a secondary air barrier system with taped and sealed 6-mil polyethylene sheets on the interior. Despite the materials chosen, all air barrier materials have to transition and terminate properly at wall to floor, wall to roof interfaces and fenestration and service penetrations to maintain the continuity of the system. The air barrier continuity can be carried with tapes or membranes that have proven long-term adhesion which should also be structurally supported on both sides or sandwiched with acoustical sealant. To comply with ASHRAE 90.1 2016, provide R-20 batt insulation by filling up the space without overly compressing the insulation. There are barn style storm doors and windows operated on a metal track system on the east and the north elevation, fastened to blocking and structural support behind. Special attention should be given to the penetrations of the track system to ensure that moisture and air barrier is continuous at these locations. 2.3 Roof Assemblies The existing roof is a low-slope roof(“flat roof”) consisting of modified bituminous roof membrane over decking, purlins, and heavy timber beams that slightly slopes toward the two eaves. The existing roof membrane has experienced damage from the recent wind storms and the roof membrane is also due for replacement as recommended by Omicron’s assessment. This can be done by furring the framing or with the insulation package. Creeping of the beams and deterioration of the timber decking has resulted in localized low areas of the roof substrate which could lead to ponding on the new roof if not addressed. 3701 Shenton Rd. Nanaimo, BC V9T 2H1 mail@heroldengineering.com (250)751-8558 Page 322 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop March 20, 2019 Page 5 of 6 4598-003 The protection board can be dispensed with if the application of the 2-ply styrene butadiene styrene (SBS) modified bituminous membrane doesn’t require open flame or the insulation is suitable to be torched against. The roof insulation is commonly staggered, rigid foam insulation boards. To reduce thermal bridging as well as avoid visible screw heads of the fasteners, a glued down system can be utilized. All membrane roof systems should be designed with demonstration that CSA A123.21 standard is met in terms of wind resistance. The added insulation would increase the depth of the roof profile and a parapet or curb structure is required at the perimeter of the roof to allow for concealment of the insulation and drainage of roof runoff with new eavestroughs. A self-adhered membrane material is recommended to provide air control over the timber decking. The air barrier membrane on the wall should be brought up and adhered to the decking and sealed around the beam penetrations. The joints in the decking directly above the wall should also be sealed with sealant to ensure a seamless air barrier transition at the wall to roof interface. 2.4 Windows and doors The existing wood frame doors and windows with single pane glass are going to be replaced with more contemporary style fenestrations with insulating glass units (IGUs). The new windows are recommended to be of thermally broken aluminum frame windows to provide long-term durability and low maintenance requirements. IGUs filled with inert gas such as argon should be used as the glazing. Furthermore, the IGUs should also have lowemissivity coating to reduce the solar heat gain. The existing windows have relatively large sizes and are fixed or hung type windows with the glass divided into a grid pattern with muntins. HEL recommends that the largest possible size of IGUs be used or restrict muntins to an external style to reduce the heat loss through the spacer. All windows and doors must conform to the North American Fenestration Standard (NAFS) and its Canadian Supplement. Glazing products such as stick-built curtain walls are currently exempt from these requirements, however, the performance criteria in part 5 of the BC Building Code still apply. With respect to windows and doors, the following standards apply: 1. Performance - Class LC – PG 25, Canadian Air Infiltration/Exfiltration Level: A3 2. Forced Entry: F2, pass test for resistance to forced entry. 3. Overall metal frame Window U-Value: maximum 2.16 W/m2•ºC based on ASHRAE 90.1-2016 . 4. Water penetration – 220 psi In instances where fenestration products are exempt from NAFS, ASTM E 783 – Field Measurement of Air Leakage Through Installed Exterior Windows and Doors and ASTM E 1105 Field Determination of Water Penetration of Installed Exterior Windows, Skylights, 3701 Shenton Rd. Nanaimo, BC V9T 2H1 mail@heroldengineering.com (250)751-8558 Page 323 of 367 Ladysmith Machine Shop March 20, 2019 Page 6 of 6 4598-003 Doors, and Curtain Walls, by Uniform or Cyclic Static Air Pressure Difference are recommended to be performed to samples of the installed fenestrations to verify the quality of the product and the install. Window shop drawings, sealed by a Professional Engineer registered in the Province of BC are required to be reviewed to establish conformance to the BC Building Code. Window install should be in conformance with the industry’s best practices with all critical barriers on the wall tied into the critical barriers of the window and the rough opening while making provisions to drainage of infiltrated water under the window sill. Flashings including head flashing with end dams are required for all the windows and doors. The configuration of the flashing is to follow CMHC’s Best Practice Guide for Flashings. Among all metal frame glazing systems, curtain wall is recommended by HEL as it is able to incorporate a pressure equalized “rain screen” system with a complete air and vapour seal. The system will allow water entering the framing to drain to the exterior and also allow air into the pressuring chamber to create pressure equalization. The window frames and integral seals should be designed to an expected service life of 30 years. All seals, gaskets, corrosion protection, coatings and attachments are expected to be serviceable at the end of this service period. 3.0 Summary and Closing Comments With the assemblies described above, the walls, roof, at-grade assemblies, and fenestrations can be designed to meet the current building code and industry’s best practices in terms of building enclosure with an expected service life of 35 to 50 years. We trust the information contained in this design brief satisfies the design team’s requirement at the moment. Should you have any questions, please contact the undersigned. EXECUTIVE SUMMARY: This report presents Bylaw 2054 for Council consideration. The proposed bylaw is a follow up to changes to sidewalk patio regulations adopted by Council earlier this year. If approved, Bylaw 2054 will amend the Streets and Traffic Bylaw to allow the Director of Development Services to authorize sidewalk patio permits during the winter months. CS 2020-169 June 2, 2020 That Council direct staff to prepare a report for the next Council meeting with a policy framework for parklet/patio spaces based on a demonstrated desire by local businesses to have such spaces available to them. INTRODUCTION/BACKGROUND: Earlier this year Council adopted bylaws clarifying regulations and eliminating fees for sidewalk patios. Since the bylaws were adopted, one new patio has been installed (Zack’s) which has been in use since August. The Streets and Traffic Bylaw allows sidewalk patios to operate from March 1st to October 31st of each year. Businesses must remove their sidewalk patios by October 31 st and cannot reinstall them until March 1st. Zack’s has requested an extension to their permit to allow the sidewalk patio to remain in place during the winter. Winter is coming. Food and beverage providers are still bound by social distancing regulations and continue to seek solutions to maintain seating capacity. In response, many jurisdictions are considering allowing winter sidewalk patios. Although Ladysmith typically enjoys much milder winters than other places in Canada, winter sidewalk patios require special, case-by-case considerations. The Town must consider traffic and pedestrian safety, accessibility, snow removal, and weather protection. Fire safety is also a major consideration, since the combination of temporary weather protection (e.g. highly flammable fabric weather barriers) and portable heat sources (which often use open flame) poses a risk if not properly managed. Not all sites will be suitable for winter sidewalk patios, however, many locations are viable and staff support creating a bylaw framework to allow winter sidewalk patios in suitable locations. PROPOSED BYLAW If Bylaw 2054 is adopted by Council, it will amend the Streets and Traffic Bylaw to allow the Director of Development Services to issue a sidewalk patio permit that authorizes a sidewalk patio to remain in place between October 31st and March 1st. The existing regulation, prohibiting patios between October 31st and March 1st will remain as the default regulation and owners will Page 349 of 367 be required to specifically request a winter sidewalk patio. The director will review the request and, assuming there are no risks associated with winter use, will authorize the patio to remain in place during the winter. DISCUSSION The south island has the warmest winter temperatures in Canada, making Ladysmith an ideal location for winter sidewalk patios. The proposed change provides an additional tool for local businesses to mitigate the impacts of the COVID-19 pandemic and supports increased vibrancy in the Downtown during the winter months. Staff are confident that health safety issues associated with winter sidewalk patios are manageable and recommend approval of the proposed bylaw. ALTERNATIVES: Council can choose to: 1. Amend Bylaw 2054 and give the bylaw first, second and third reading as amended. 2. Refer Bylaw 2054 back to staff for further review as specified by Council. 3. Reject Bylaw 2054. FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS None LEGAL IMPLICATIONS: None CITIZEN/PUBLIC RELATIONS IMPLICATIONS: Public consultation is not required to consider or adopt Bylaw 2054. INTERDEPARTMENTAL INVOLVEMENT/IMPLICATIONS: Sidewalk patio applications are referred to relevant departments for review and comment. This practice will continue if Bylaw 2054 is approved. INTRODUCTION/BACKGROUND: This downtown washroom project has been in the works for many years. COVID-19 and the downtown patio table project has shown the necessity of a public washroom on 1 st Avenue. A temporary washroom was installed where the proposed permanent facility will be placed and, although negative feedback from one neighbouring business was received, overall the response to a washroom facility has been positive. This project is a community group (Kinsmen) led project. Though the scope of this facility has changed over time, the latest version of the project is expected to cost approximately $100k. The Town has committed $32k from the Real Property Reserve and the remaining funding is from donations, grants and Kinsmen fundraising. Normally, the Town would follow the Purchasing Policy in obtaining quotes and proposals. Since the downtown washroom is a community group project, it is requested that Council Page 355 of 367 amend the project budget to $100,000 (from $150,000) and waive the purchasing policy, which will allow the community group to select suppliers and bidders. The design of the washroom will be brought to the necessary committees for approval and the official closing of the alley, by bylaw, will be presented to Council. ALTERNATIVES: Council can choose to: 1. Not accept the donation from the Kinsmen for the washroom project. 2. Re-submit a grant application and wait for a funding decision. 3. Continue to use a portable washroom in the location. FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS; Due to a scope change, the cost of the project is decreasing. The Town has committed to $32k from the Real Property Reserve for this project. There is an operation cost associated with a permanent facility in the amount of $26k to cover the servicing and maintenance. A portion of this ($15k) was included in the 2020-2024 Financial Plan. LEGAL IMPLICATIONS; As this is a community group led project, waiving the purchasing policy allows the community group to select the vendor. CITIZEN/PUBLIC RELATIONS IMPLICATIONS: There was one business that voiced their concerns regarding the temporary washroom. Many other businesses and visitors to the downtown support the proposal. INTERDEPARTMENTAL INVOLVEMENT/IMPLICATIONS: This project impacts multiple departments. Development Services will oversee the design through the approval process; Public Works will oversee the site servicing, and Parks and Recreation will oversee the maintenance. Erin Anderson, Acting Chief Administrative Officer ATTACHMENT(S): Page 357 of 367 STAFF REPORT TO COUNCIL Report Prepared By: Meeting Date: File No: RE: Chris Barfoot, Director Parks, Recreation and Culture October 6, 2020 RE-OPENING PLAN FOR SWIMMING POOL RECOMMENDATION: That Council direct staff to: 1. Reopen the 25m pool and the therapy teach pool at the Frank Jameson Community Centre on November 2nd with limited programming as presented by the Director of Parks, Recreation and Culture; and 2. Phase in the opening of the other pool amenities when permitted by the Provincial Health Officer and other regulatory agencies. EXECUTIVE SUMMARY: All Town recreation facilities were closed on March 16 th, 2020. From that time, most outdoor facilities and many of the components of the Frank Jameson Community Centre (FJCC) have reopened with the exception of the swimming pool, due to the complexities and strict guidelines resulting from COVID-19. Town staff have developed plans, protocols and detailed re-opening strategies necessary to keep everyone safe and healthy while the facility is open and operating. PREVIOUS COUNCIL DIRECTION N/A INTRODUCTION/BACKGROUND: On March 16th, 2020 the Town of Ladysmith’s Parks, Recreation and Culture Department closed all recreation facilities for programs, rentals and drop-in activities. These measures were taken to safeguard the public health of our residents, in accordance with the Provincial Health Officer’s order to cancel gatherings of more than 50 people and to practice physical distancing of a minimum of 2 meters, to help prevent the spread of COVID-19. Closing these facilities had an immediate impact on the community, especially those residents who rely on the services and programs offered by the Parks, Recreation and Culture Department. Since that time, most outdoor amenities and a limited offering of indoor activities at FJCC have re-opened. Outdoor programs, including some local sport organizations, have also resumed activity. Facility rentals are carefully being processed for essential services, and where safe to do so, non-essential services such as community meetings where groups are seeking access to larger facilities to comply with physical distancing. Currently, there are a limited Page 358 of 367 number of indoor programs at FJCC that have resumed using a phased approach as recommended by the Provincial Health Officer, WorkSafe BC and the British Columbia Recreation and Parks Association (BCRPA). In order to re-open the remaining components of the indoor facilities, Town staff have been working through complex and detailed re-opening procedures and scheduling scenarios for each component of FJCC in order to ensure public and participants are kept safe and healthy while moving throughout the building as the facility and programs continue to re-open. In the event of an increase in COVID-19 reported cases, a phased in approach providing the department the ability to scale back and adjust as necessary. RE-OPENING PLAN FOR SWIMMING POOL In order to meet current orders, guidelines and recommendations the pool schedule has been modified to ensure physical distancing in and around the swimming pool. Maximum group size numbers (based on the recommended space required for each participant) have been established as to accommodate participants in all areas including the pool, pool deck, change rooms and hallways. The proposed plan includes:  Opening the 25m pool and the therapy teach pool (the hot tub and sauna remain closed).  Providing 10 hours of aquatic fitness per week  Allowing length swimming for a maximum of 12 per session, using the same reservation system as the fitness centre.  Allowing for the local swim club to rent 7.5 hours per week for their swim practice  Eventually offering swim lessons This will impact FJCC swimming pool operations in the following ways: FJCC Public Swims Swim Club Rentals Aquatic Fitness Swim Lessons Normal Operation (pre COVID-19) Hours for Nov - Dec 511 86 154 203 144 Modified Operation Phase 1 Hours for Nov – Dec * 101** 52.5 175 90 Still developing safety plans *This does not include Christmas Swim Schedule **Hot Tub and Sauna will remain closed in Phase 1 When permitted, in future, the number of participants and additional classes can be phased-in. Using a phased approach will provide staff the ability to monitor the appropriate numbers and space management. It will allow the plan to expand or contract services being offered as regulations and recommendations change. Staff will be impacted FINANCIAL IMPLICATIONS; Back in March when the budget was being re-adjusted due to COVID-19, it was assumed that the pool would be back in operation by September and revenues would be generated as per usual. Since this is not the case, adjustments to the operating planned revenues are required. One of the largest impacts is the reduced capacity permitted. Before, there could easily be 25 participants in an aqua fit session and 10 swimmers in each length swimming session of the pool for a 67% cost recovery for the operation. With the restriction of the bather loads/session, there is a significant reduction in achievable revenue. There is also an additional cost associated with sanitizing the pool equipment and change rooms between each session. For these reasons, staff are proposing a program fee of $6.50 for each registered booking. This will allow for a cost recovery of 51% for the 9 weeks from November 2 to December 18, 2020 (see appendix A) LEGAL IMPLICATIONS; N/A CITIZEN/PUBLIC RELATIONS IMPLICATIONS: Community members have strongly expressed that they not only want, but need, to get back to activities that support their physical, mental and social well-being. The swimming pool has played an integral component to their activities and keeping it closed may raise their frustrations. The re-opening plan for the swimming pool has been carefully planned using the same strict guidelines, procedures and process as the other programs and based on the following assumptions:  Community members not only want, but need, to get back to activities that support their physical, mental and social well-being.  Many of the regular pool users cannot access land-based activities and have been unable to access recreation since the pool closure.
